<p><b>Sharia</b>, <b>Sharia law</b>, or <b>Islamic law</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl"><span style="font-size:large;">شريعة</span></span>&#8206; (<small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʃaˈriːʕa]</a></span>)) is a <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious law</a> forming part of the <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-oxforddic_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddic-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It is derived from the religious precepts of Islam, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, the term <i>sharīʿah</i> refers to <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>'s immutable <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine law</a> and is contrasted with <i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i>, which refers to its human scholarly interpretations.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It has been described as "one of the major intellectual achievements of Islam"<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis-ME-223_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-ME-223-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and its importance in Islam has been compared to that of <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">theology</a> in Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> The manner of its application in modern times has been a subject of dispute between Muslim traditionalists and reformists.<sup id="cite_ref-oxforddic_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddic-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">theory of Islamic jurisprudence</a> recognizes four <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_sharia" title="Sources of sharia">sources of sharia</a>: the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i> (authentic hadith), <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a></i> (analogical reasoning), and <i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijma</a></i> (juridical consensus).<sup id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2001_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2001-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Different <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">legal schools</a>—of which the most prominent are <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ja%27fari_jurisprudence" title="Ja&#39;fari jurisprudence">Jafari</a>—developed methodologies for deriving sharia rulings from scriptural sources using a process known as <i><a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Traditional jurisprudence</a> distinguishes two principal branches of law, <i><a href="/wiki/Ibadah" title="Ibadah">ʿibādāt</a></i> (rituals) and <i><a href="/wiki/Muamalat" title="Muamalat">muʿāmalāt</a></i> (social relations), which together comprise a wide range of topics.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Its rulings assign actions to one of <a href="/wiki/Ahkam" title="Ahkam">five categories</a>: <a href="/wiki/Fard" title="Fard">mandatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustahabb" title="Mustahabb">recommended</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mubah" title="Mubah">neutral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makruh" title="Makruh">abhorred</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">prohibited</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Thus, some areas of sharia overlap with the Western notion of law while others correspond more broadly to living life in accordance with God’s will.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, sharia was interpreted by independent jurists (<a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">muftis</a>). Their legal opinions (<a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwas</a>) were taken into account by ruler-appointed <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">judges</a> who presided over <i><a href="/wiki/Q%C4%81%E1%B8%8D%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Qāḍī">qāḍī</a>'s</i> courts, and by <i>maẓālim</i> courts, which were controlled by the ruler's council and administered criminal law.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman empire">Ottoman rulers</a> achieved additional control over the legal system by promulgating their own legal code (<i><a href="/wiki/Qanun_(law)" title="Qanun (law)">qanun</a></i>) and turning muftis into state employees.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Non-Muslim (<a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">dhimmi</a>) communities had legal autonomy, except in cases of interconfessional disputes, which fell under jurisdiction of qadi's courts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern era, sharia-based criminal laws have been widely replaced by statutes inspired by European models.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto200819_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto200819-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Judicial procedures and legal education in the Muslim world were likewise brought in line with European practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> While the constitutions of most Muslim-majority states contain references to sharia, its classical rules were largely retained only in <a href="/wiki/Status_(law)" title="Status (law)">personal status</a> (family) laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Legislative bodies which codified these laws sought to modernize them without abandoning their foundations in traditional jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayer2009_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayer2009-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a> of the late 20th century brought along calls by <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> movements for full implementation of sharia, including reinstatement of <i><a href="/wiki/Hudud" title="Hudud">hudud</a></i> corporal punishments, such as <a href="/wiki/Stoning" title="Stoning">stoning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayer2009_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayer2009-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, this resulted in traditionalist legal reform,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> while other countries witnessed juridical reinterpretation of sharia advocated by progressive reformers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayer2009_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayer2009-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The role of sharia has become a contested topic around the world. Attempts to impose it on non-Muslims have caused <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence_in_Nigeria" title="Religious violence in Nigeria">intercommunal violence in Nigeria</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and may have contributed to the breakup of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Some Muslim-minority countries in Asia (such as <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>), Africa, and Europe recognize the use of sharia-based family laws for their Muslim populations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto200818–20_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto200818–20-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Some jurisdictions in North America have passed <a href="/wiki/Ban_on_sharia_law" title="Ban on sharia law">bans on use of sharia</a>, framed as restrictions on religious or foreign laws.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> There are ongoing debates as to whether sharia is compatible with secular forms of <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of thought</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">women's rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hajjar2004_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hajjar2004-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">Etymology and usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_usage">Contemporary usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Contemporary usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>sharīʿah</i> is used by Arabic-speaking peoples of the Middle East to designate a prophetic religion in its totality.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> For example, <i>sharīʿat Mūsā</i> means law or religion of Moses and <i>sharīʿatu-nā</i> can mean "our religion" in reference to any monotheistic faith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Within Islamic discourse, <i>šarīʿah</i> refers to religious regulations governing the lives of Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> For many Muslims, the word merely means "justice," and they will consider any law that promotes justice and social welfare to conform to sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jan Michiel Otto distinguishes four senses conveyed by the term <i>sharia</i> in religious, legal and political discourse:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto20089–10_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto20089–10-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Divine, abstract sharia</i>: God's plan for mankind and the norms of behavior which should guide the Islamic community. Muslims of different perspectives agree in their respect for the abstract notion of sharia, but they differ in how they understand the practical implications of the term.</li>
<li><i>Classical sharia</i>: the body of rules and principles elaborated by Islamic jurists during the first centuries of Islam.</li>
<li><i>Historical sharia(s)</i>: the body of rules and interpretations developed throughout Islamic history, ranging from personal beliefs to state legislation and varying across an ideological spectrum. Classical sharia has often served as a point of reference for these variants, but they have also reflected the influences of their time and place.</li>
<li><i>Contemporary sharia(s)</i>: the full spectrum of rules and interpretations that are developed and practiced at present.</li></ul>
<p>A related term <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration">al-qānūn al-islāmī</i></i> (<span lang="ar" title="Arabic language text"><span style="font-size:large;">القانون الإسلامي</span></span>, Islamic law), which was borrowed from European usage in the late 19th century, is used in the Muslim world to refer to a legal system in the context of a modern state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007323_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007323-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary range of meanings of the Arabic word <i>šarīʿah</i>, derived from the root <i>š-r-ʕ</i>, is related to religion and religious law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The lexicographical tradition records two major areas of use where the word <i>šarīʿah</i> can appear without religious connotation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In texts evoking a pastoral or nomadic environment, the word, and its derivatives refer to watering animals at a permanent water-hole or to the seashore, with special reference to animals who come there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Another area of use relates to notions of stretched or lengthy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This range of meanings is cognate with the Hebrew <i>saraʿ</i> and is likely to be the origin of the meaning "way" or "path".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Both these areas have been claimed to have Within rise to aspects of the religious meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007326-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars describe the word <i>šarīʿah</i> as an archaic <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> word denoting "pathway to be followed" (analogous to the Hebrew term <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakhah</a> ["The Way to Go"]),<sup id="cite_ref-haqq_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haqq-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> or "path to the water hole"<sup id="cite_ref-Hashim1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hashim1-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weiss1998_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weiss1998-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and argue that its adoption as a metaphor for a divinely ordained way of life arises from the importance of water in an arid desert environment.<sup id="cite_ref-weiss1998_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weiss1998-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_religious_texts">Use in religious texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Use in religious texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Quran, <i>šarīʿah</i> and its cognate <i>širʿah</i> occur once each, with the meaning "way" or "path".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The word <i>šarīʿah</i> was widely used by Arabic-speaking Jews during the Middle Ages, being the most common translation for the word <i>torah</i> in the 10th-century Arabic Old Testament known as Saʿadya Gaon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> A similar use of the term can be found in Christian writers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007321-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The Arabic expression <i>Sharīʿat Allāh</i> (<span lang="ar" dir="ltr">شريعة الله</span> "God’s Law") is a common translation for תורת אלוהים (‘God’s Law’ in Hebrew) and νόμος τοῦ θεοῦ (‘God’s Law’ in Greek in the New Testament [Rom. 7: 22]).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In Muslim literature, <i>šarīʿah</i> designates the laws or message of a prophet or God, in contrast to <i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i>, which refers to a scholar's interpretation thereof.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007322_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalderHooker2007322-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_origins">Historical origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Historical origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">Madhhab</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">Ahl al-Hadith</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibnhanbal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ibnhanbal.jpg/220px-Ibnhanbal.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ibnhanbal.jpg/330px-Ibnhanbal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Ibnhanbal.jpg/440px-Ibnhanbal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ibnhanbal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juristic exchange between <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dawood" title="Abu Dawood">Abu Dawood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hanbal" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Hanbal">Ibn Hanbal</a>. One of the oldest literary manuscripts of the Islamic world, dated October 879.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the traditional Muslim view, there was no "historical development" of Islamic law and its major precepts were all known and passed down directly from the Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Forte-1978-8_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forte-1978-8-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The emergence of Islamic jurisprudence (<i>fiqh</i>) also goes back to the lifetime of Muhammad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In this view, his <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">companions</a> and followers took what he did and approved of as a model (<a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a>) and transmitted this information to the succeeding generations in the form of <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> These reports led first to informal discussion and then systematic legal thought, articulated with greatest success in the eighth and ninth centuries by the master jurists <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifah" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Hanifah">Abu Hanifah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Shafi%E2%80%98i" title="Al-Shafi‘i">Al-Shafi‘i</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>, who are viewed as the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shafi%CA%BFi" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafiʿi">Shafiʿi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> legal schools (<i><a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">madhhabs</a></i>) of Sunni jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern historians have presented alternative theories of the formation of fiqh.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> At first Western scholars accepted the general outlines of the traditional account.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In the late 19th century, an influential <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist</a> hypothesis was advanced by <a href="/wiki/Ignac_Goldziher" class="mw-redirect" title="Ignac Goldziher">Ignac Goldziher</a> and elaborated by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schacht" title="Joseph Schacht">Joseph Schacht</a> in the mid-20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Schacht and other scholars<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> argued that the local practices of early Muslim communities (which Schacht calls the `living tradition` of the `ancient schools of law`) and earliest efforts at Islamic jurisprudence<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> were based on a variety of sources, not just the ahadith of Muhammad.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup>
Furthermore, most hadith and their <a href="/wiki/Hadith_studies#Sanad_and_matn" title="Hadith studies">chains of transmission</a> were actually created at a later date <sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup> 
and extended back to Muhammad's companions (despite the efforts of hadith scholars to uncover fabricated isnads), when it became accepted that legal norms must be formally grounded in scriptural sources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In his view, the real architect of Islamic jurisprudence was al-Shafi'i, who formulated this and other elements of classical legal theory in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Risala_(Al-Shafi%E2%80%98i)" title="Al-Risala (Al-Shafi‘i)">al-risala</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> but who was preceded by a body of Islamic law not based on primacy of ahadith of  Muhammad. (Muhammad died in 632 CE while Al-Shafi’i began to establish an independent line of legal speculation in 810.) These accounts gave rise to objections, and modern historians generally adopt more cautious, intermediate positions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJokisch2015-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the origin of hadith remains a subject of scholarly controversy, it is generally accepted that early Islamic jurisprudence developed out of a combination of administrative and popular practices shaped by the religious and ethical precepts of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> It continued some aspects of pre-Islamic laws and customs of the lands that fell under Muslim rule in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early conquests</a> and modified other aspects, aiming to meet the practical need of establishing Islamic norms of behavior and adjudicating disputes arising in the early Muslim communities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapidus2014125_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapidus2014125-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Juristic thought gradually developed in study circles, where independent scholars met to learn from a local master and discuss religious topics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapidus2014125_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapidus2014125-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> At first, these circles were fluid in their membership, but with time distinct regional legal schools crystallized around shared sets of methodological principles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As the boundaries of the schools became clearly delineated, the authority of their doctrinal tenets came to be vested in a master jurist from earlier times, who was henceforth identified as the school's founder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200931–35-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the course of the first three centuries of Islam, all legal schools came to accept the broad outlines of classical legal theory, according to which Islamic law had to be firmly rooted in the Quran and hadith.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapidus2014130_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapidus2014130-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Traditional_jurisprudence_.28fiqh.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_jurisprudence_(fiqh)">Traditional jurisprudence (<i>fiqh</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Traditional jurisprudence (fiqh)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a></div>
<p>Fiqh is traditionally divided into the fields of <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">uṣūl al-fiqh</a></i> (lit. the roots of fiqh), which studies the theoretical principles of jurisprudence, and <i>furūʿ al-fiqh</i> (lit. the branches of fiqh), which is devoted to elaboration of rulings on the basis of these principles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2014_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2014-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Principles_of_jurisprudence_.28u.E1.B9.A3.C5.ABl_al-fiqh.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles_of_jurisprudence_(uṣūl_al-fiqh)">Principles of jurisprudence (<i>uṣūl al-fiqh</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Principles of jurisprudence (uṣūl al-fiqh)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">Principles of Islamic jurisprudence</a></div>
<p>Classical jurists held that human reason is a gift from God which should be exercised to its fullest capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200915_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200915-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, they believed that use of reason alone is insufficient to distinguish right from wrong, and that rational argumentation must draw its content from the body of transcendental knowledge revealed in the Quran and through the sunnah of Muhammad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200915_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200915-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional theory of Islamic jurisprudence elaborates how scriptures should be interpreted from the standpoint of linguistics and rhetoric.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It also comprises methods for establishing authenticity of hadith and for determining when the legal force of a scriptural passage is <a href="/wiki/Naskh_(tafsir)" title="Naskh (tafsir)">abrogated</a> by a passage revealed at a later date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the Quran and sunnah, the classical theory of Sunni fiqh recognizes two other sources of law: juristic consensus (<i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijmaʿ</a></i>) and analogical reasoning (<i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> It therefore studies the application and limits of analogy, as well as the value and limits of consensus, along with other methodological principles, some of which are accepted by only certain legal schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This interpretive apparatus is brought together under the rubric of <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>, which refers to a jurist's exertion in an attempt to arrive at a ruling on a particular question.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The theory of <a href="/wiki/Twelver_Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelver Shia">Twelver Shia</a> jurisprudence parallels that of Sunni schools with some differences, such as recognition of reason (<i><a href="/wiki/%27Aql" title="&#39;Aql">ʿaql</a></i>) as a source of law in place of <i>qiyas</i> and extension of the notion of sunnah to include traditions of the <a href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">imams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamali1999121–22_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamali1999121–22-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_sharia">Sources of sharia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sources of sharia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_sharia" title="Sources of sharia">Sources of sharia</a></div>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a></i>: In Islam, the Quran is considered to be the most sacred source of law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Classical jurists held its textual integrity to be beyond doubt on account of it having been handed down by many people in each generation, which is known as "recurrence" or "concurrent transmission" (<i>tawātur</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Only several hundred verses of the Quran have direct legal relevance, and they are concentrated in a few specific areas such as inheritance, though other passages have been used as a source for general principles whose legal ramifications were elaborated by other means.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a></i>: The body of hadith provides more detailed and practical legal guidance, but it was recognized early on that not all of them were authentic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Early Islamic scholars developed a methodology for evaluating their authenticity by assessing trustworthiness of the individuals listed in their transmission chains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> These criteria narrowed down the vast corpus of prophetic traditions to several thousand "sound" hadiths, which were collected in several canonical compilations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The hadiths which enjoyed concurrent transmission were deemed unquestionably authentic; however, the vast majority of hadiths were handed down by only one or a few transmitters and were therefore seen to yield only probable knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916-18_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916-18-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The uncertainty was further compounded by ambiguity of the language contained in some hadiths and Quranic passages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18_52-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200916–18-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Disagreements on the relative merits and interpretation of the textual sources allowed legal scholars considerable leeway in formulating alternative rulings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Consensus (<a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijma</a>)</i> could in principle elevate a ruling based on probable evidence to absolute certainty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This classical doctrine drew its authority from a series of hadiths stating that the Islamic community could never agree on an error.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This form of consensus was technically defined as agreement of all competent jurists in any particular generation, acting as representatives of the community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKamali1999146_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKamali1999146-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> However, the practical difficulty of obtaining and ascertaining such an agreement meant that it had little impact on legal development.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> A more pragmatic form of consensus, which could be determined by consulting works of prominent jurists, was used to confirm a ruling so that it could not be reopened for further discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The cases for which there was a consensus account for less than 1 percent of the body of classical jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22_54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200921–22-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Analogical reasoning (<a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a>) </i>: Qiyas is used to derive a ruling for a situation not addressed in the scripture by analogy with a scripturally based rule.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In a classic example, the Quranic prohibition of drinking wine is extended to all intoxicating substances, on the basis of the "cause"  (<i>ʿilla</i>) shared by these situations, which in this case is identified to be intoxication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Since the cause of a rule may not be apparent, its selection commonly occasioned controversy and extensive debate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200923–24_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200923–24-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Twelver Shia jurisprudence does not recognize the use of qiyas, but relies on reason (<i>ʿaql</i>) in its place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2014_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2014-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ijtihad">Ijtihad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ijtihad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">Ijtihad</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg/220px-T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg" width="220" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg/330px-T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg/440px-T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="1803" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BCrkischer_Mufti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turkish mufti (17th-century Spanish drawing)</div></div></div>
<p>The classical process of ijtihad combined these generally recognized principles with other methods, which were not adopted by all legal schools, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Istihsan" title="Istihsan">istihsan</a></i> (juristic preference), <i><a href="/wiki/Istislah" title="Istislah">istislah</a></i> (consideration of public interest) and <i><a href="/wiki/Istishab" title="Istishab">istishab</a></i> (presumption of continuity).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A jurist who is qualified to practice ijtihad is known as a <i>mujtahid</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The use of independent reasoning to arrive at a ruling is contrasted with <i><a href="/wiki/Taqlid" title="Taqlid">taqlid</a></i> (imitation), which refers to following the rulings of a mujtahid.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> By the beginning of the 10th century, development of Sunni jurisprudence prompted leading jurists to state that the main legal questions had been addressed and the scope of ijtihad was gradually restricted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009c_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009c-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> From the 18th century on, leading Muslim reformers began calling for abandonment of taqlid and renewed emphasis on ijtihad, which they saw as a return to the vitality of early Islamic jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009c_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009c-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Decision_types_.28a.E1.B8.A5k.C4.81m.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decision_types_(aḥkām)">Decision types (<i>aḥkām</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decision types (aḥkām)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ahkam" title="Ahkam">Ahkam</a></div>
<p>Sharia rulings fall into one of five categories known as “the five decisions” (<i>al-aḥkām al-khamsa</i>): <a href="/wiki/Fard" title="Fard">mandatory</a> (<i>farḍ</i> or <i>wājib</i>), <a href="/wiki/Mustahabb" title="Mustahabb">recommended</a> (<i>mandūb</i> or <i>mustaḥabb</i>), <a href="/wiki/Mubah" title="Mubah">neutral</a> (<i>mubāḥ</i>), <a href="/wiki/Makruh" title="Makruh">reprehensible</a> (<i>makrūh</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">forbidden</a> (<i>ḥarām</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2014_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2014-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It is a sin or a crime to perform a forbidden action or not to perform a mandatory action.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Reprehensible acts should be avoided, but they are not considered to be sinful or punishable in court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200920_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200920-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Avoiding reprehensible acts and performing recommended acts is held to be subject of reward in the afterlife, while allowed actions entail no judgement from God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200920_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200920-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Jurists disagree on whether the term <i><a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">ḥalāl</a></i> covers the first three or the first four categories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The legal and moral verdict depends on whether the action is committed out of necessity (<i>ḍarūra</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aims_of_sharia_and_public_interest">Aims of sharia and public interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Aims of sharia and public interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Maqasid" title="Maqasid">Maqasid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maslaha" title="Maslaha">Maslaha</a></div>
<p><i>Maqāṣid</i> (aims or purposes) of sharia  and <i>maṣlaḥa</i> (welfare or public interest) are two related classical doctrines which have come to play an increasingly prominent role in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2009_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2009-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleave2012_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleave2012-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> They were first clearly articulated by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a> (d. 1111), who argued that <i>maslaha</i> was God's general purpose in revealing the divine law, and that its specific aim was preservation of five essentials of human well-being: religion, life, intellect, offspring, and property.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOpwis200765_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOpwis200765-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Although most classical-era jurists recognized <i>maslaha</i> and <i>maqasid</i> as important legal principles, they held different views regarding the role they should play in Islamic law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleave2012_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleave2012-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Some jurists viewed them as auxiliary rationales constrained by scriptural sources and analogical reasoning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOpwis200766–68_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOpwis200766–68-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Others regarded them as an independent source of law, whose general principles could override specific inferences based on the letter of scripture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOpwis200768–69_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOpwis200768–69-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> While the latter view was held by a minority of classical jurists, in modern times it came to be championed in different forms by prominent scholars who sought to adapt Islamic law to changing social conditions by drawing on the intellectual heritage of traditional jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009_44-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2009_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2009-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> These scholars expanded the inventory of <i>maqasid</i> to include such aims of sharia as reform and women's rights (<a href="/wiki/Rashid_Rida" title="Rashid Rida">Rashid Rida</a>); justice and freedom (<a href="/wiki/Mohammed_al-Ghazali" title="Mohammed al-Ghazali">Mohammed al-Ghazali</a>); and human dignity and rights (<a href="/wiki/Yusuf_al-Qaradawi" title="Yusuf al-Qaradawi">Yusuf al-Qaradawi</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6_59-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuderija20142–6-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches_of_law">Branches of law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Branches of law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:lavender;padding-left:0.4em;text-align:center;padding-right:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Islamic_criminal_jurisprudence" title="Islamic criminal jurisprudence">Criminal</a></div><div class="NavContent plainlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:1.5em;padding-right:1.5em;"><table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;border-collapse:collapse; border-spacing:0px; border:none; width:100%; margin:0px; font-size:100%; clear:none; float:none"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">

</tr></tbody></table></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:lavender;padding-left:0.4em;text-align:center;padding-right:0.4em;"><a href="/wiki/Islamic_military_jurisprudence" title="Islamic military jurisprudence">Military</a></div><div class="NavContent plainlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:1.5em;padding-right:1.5em;">

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.1em;">
<a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic studies</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Fiqh" title="Template:Fiqh"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Fiqh" title="Template talk:Fiqh"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fiqh&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Topics_of_sharia_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Topics of sharia law">Topics of sharia law</a></div>
<p>The domain of <i>furūʿ al-fiqh</i> (lit. branches of fiqh) is traditionally divided into <i>ʿibādāt</i> (rituals or acts of worship) and <i>muʿāmalāt</i> (social relations).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Many jurists further divided the body of substantive jurisprudence into "the four quarters", called rituals, sales, marriage and injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Each of these terms figuratively stood for a variety of subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> For example, the quarter of sales would encompass partnerships, guaranty, gifts, and bequests, among other topics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Juristic works were arranged as a sequence of such smaller topics, each called a "book" (<i>kitab</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The special significance of ritual was marked by always placing its discussion at the start of the work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30_65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200928-30-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians distinguish a field of Islamic criminal law, which combines several traditional categories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2014_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2014-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Several crimes with scripturally prescribed punishments are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Hudud" title="Hudud">hudud</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Jurists developed various restrictions which in many cases made them virtually impossible to apply.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Other crimes involving intentional bodily harm are judged according to a version of <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_talionis" class="mw-redirect" title="Lex talionis">lex talionis</a></i> that prescribes a punishment analogous to the crime (<i><a href="/wiki/Qisas" title="Qisas">qisas</a></i>), but the victims or their heirs may accept a monetary compensation (<i><a href="/wiki/Diya_(Islam)" title="Diya (Islam)">diya</a></i>) or pardon the perpetrator instead; only <i>diya</i> is imposed for non-intentional harm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Other criminal cases belong to the category of <i><a href="/wiki/Tazir" title="Tazir">taʿzīr</a></i>, where the goal of punishment is correction or rehabilitation of the culprit and its form is largely left to the judge's discretion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In practice, since early on in Islamic history, criminal cases were usually handled by ruler-administered courts or local police using procedures which were only loosely related to sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiadeh2009c-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two major genres of <i>furūʿ</i> literature are the <i>mukhtasar</i> (concise summary of law) and the <i>mabsut</i> (extensive commentary).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  <i>Mukhtasars</i> were short specialized treatises or general overviews that could be used in a classroom or consulted by judges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A <i>mabsut</i>, which usually provided a commentary on a <i>mukhtasar</i> and could stretch to dozens of large volumes, recorded alternative rulings with their justifications, often accompanied by a proliferation of cases and conceptual distinctions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The terminology of juristic literature was conservative and tended to preserve notions which had lost their practical relevance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, the cycle of abridgement and commentary allowed jurists of each generation to articulate a modified body of law to meet changing social conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHallaq200910-11-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Other juristic genres include the <i>qawāʿid</i> (succinct formulas meant to aid the student remember general principles) and collections of fatwas by a particular scholar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_of_law">Schools of law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Schools of law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">Madhhab</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/300px-Madhhab_Map3.png" width="300" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/450px-Madhhab_Map3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/600px-Madhhab_Map3.png 2x" data-file-width="1245" data-file-height="604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Predominant madhhab by region of the Muslim world</div></div></div>
<p>The main Sunni schools of law (<i>madhhabs</i>) are the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> madhhabs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> They emerged in the ninth and tenth centuries and by the twelfth century almost all jurists aligned themselves with a particular madhhab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> These four schools recognize each other's validity and they have interacted in legal debate over the centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Rulings of these schools are followed across the Muslim world without exclusive regional restrictions, but they each came to dominate in different parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  For example, the Maliki school is predominant in North and West Africa; the Hanafi school in South and Central Asia; the Shafi'i school in Lower Egypt, East Africa, and Southeast Asia; and the Hanbali school in North and Central Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The first centuries of Islam also witnessed a number of short-lived Sunni madhhabs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zahiri" class="mw-redirect" title="Zahiri">Zahiri</a> school, which is commonly identified as extinct, continues to exert influence over legal thought.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The development of Shia legal schools occurred along the lines of theological differences and resulted in formation of the <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> madhhabs, whose differences from Sunni legal schools are roughly of the same order as the differences among Sunni schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder2009_4-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a> legal school, distinct from Sunni and Shia madhhabs, is predominant in Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERabb2009b_45-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERabb2009b-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The transformations of Islamic legal institutions in the modern era have had profound implications for the madhhab system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  Legal practice in most of the Muslim world has come to be controlled by government policy and state law, so that the influence of the madhhabs beyond personal ritual practice depends on the status accorded to them within the national legal system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> State law codification commonly utilized the methods of <i>takhayyur</i> (selection of rulings without restriction to a particular madhhab) and <i>talfiq</i> (combining parts of different rulings on the same question).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Legal professionals trained in modern law schools have largely replaced traditional <a href="/wiki/Ulema" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulema">ulema</a> as interpreters of the resulting laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Global Islamic movements have at times drawn on different madhhabs and at other times placed greater focus on the scriptural sources rather than classical jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The Hanbali school, with its particularly strict adherence to the Quran and hadith, has inspired conservative currents of direct scriptural interpretation by the <a href="/wiki/Salafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> movements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Other currents, such as networks of Indonesian ulema and Islamic scholars residing in Muslim-minority countries, have advanced liberal interpretations of Islamic law without focusing on traditions of a particular madhhab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussin2014_68-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussin2014-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p><a href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg" class="image"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="215" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam&#32;portal</a>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-right:0.2em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islamism_sidebar" title="Template:Islamism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Islamism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Islamism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Islamism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><br />
From the 9th century onward, the power to interpret law in traditional Islamic societies was in the hands of the scholars (<a href="/wiki/Ulema" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulema">ulema</a>). This separation of powers served to limit the range of actions available to the ruler, who could not easily decree or reinterpret law independently and expect the continued support of the community.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Through succeeding centuries and empires, the balance between the ulema and the rulers shifted and reformed, but the balance of power was never decisively changed.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Over the course of many centuries, imperial, political and technological change, including the Industrial Revolution and the French Revolution, ushered in an era of European world <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> that gradually included the domination of many of the lands which had previously been ruled by Islamic empires.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the Second World War, the European powers found themselves too weakened to maintain their empires as before.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The wide variety of forms of government, systems of law, attitudes toward modernity and interpretations of sharia are a result of the ensuing drives for independence and modernity in the Muslim world.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Application">Application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section  <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span>  <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_by_country">Application by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Application by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Application_of_sharia_law_by_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Application of sharia law by country">Application of sharia law by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg/300px-Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg.png" width="300" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg/450px-Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg/600px-Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Use_of_Sharia_by_country.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Use of sharia by country:<br /> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #179C86; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Sharia plays no role in the judicial system.</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F6DD4F; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Sharia applies to Muslim's personal law.</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #706EA4; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Sharia applies in full, including criminal law.</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF9950; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Regional variations in the application of sharia.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Most Muslim-majority countries incorporate sharia at some level in their legal framework, with many calling it the highest law or the source of law of the land in their constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-tjmas_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tjmas-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Most use sharia for personal law (marriage, divorce, domestic violence, child support, family law, inheritance and such matters).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhadduriLiebesny1955&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2016&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhadduriLiebesny1955[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2016]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Elements of sharia are present, to varying extents, in the criminal justice system of many Muslim-majority countries.<sup id="cite_ref-nabiad_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nabiad-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> apply the code predominantly or entirely while it applies in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nabiad_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nabiad-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto201028_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto201028-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
