</p><p>Most Muslim-majority countries with sharia-prescribed <a href="/wiki/Hudud" title="Hudud">hudud</a> punishments in their legal code do not prescribe it routinely and use other punishments instead.<sup id="cite_ref-tjmas_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tjmas-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rightto_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rightto-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The harshest sharia penalties such as stoning, <a href="/wiki/Beheading" class="mw-redirect" title="Beheading">beheading</a> and other forms of the death penalty are enforced with varying levels of consistency.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1970s, most Muslim-majority countries have faced vociferous demands from their religious groups and political parties for immediate adoption of sharia as the sole, or at least primary, legal framework.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Some moderates and liberal scholars within these Muslim-majority countries have argued for limited expansion of sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto201029–33_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto201029–33-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the growing Muslim immigrant communities in Western Europe, there have been reports in some media of "no-go zones" being established where sharia law reigns supreme.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> However, there is no evidence of the existence of "no-go zones", and these allegations are sourced from anti-immigrant groups falsely equating low-income neighborhoods predominantly inhabited by immigrants with "no-go zones".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In England, the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Arbitration_Tribunal" title="Muslim Arbitration Tribunal">Muslim Arbitration Tribunal</a> makes use of sharia family law to settle disputes, though this limited adoption of sharia is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforcement">Enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_religious_police" title="Islamic religious police">Islamic religious police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hisbah" title="Hisbah">Hisbah</a></div>
<p>Sharia is enforced in Muslim-majority nations in a number of ways, including <i>mutaween</i> (police enforcement) and <i>hisbah</i>. <a href="/wiki/Mutaween" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutaween">mutaween</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl"><span style="font-size:large;">المطوعين، مطوعية</span></span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language transliteration">muṭawwiʿīn, muṭawwiʿiyyah</i></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> are the government-authorized or government-recognized religious police (or clerical <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. Elsewhere, enforcement of Islamic values in accordance with sharia is the responsibility of the <i>Polisi Perda Syariah Islam</i> in Aceh province of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Propagation_of_Virtue_and_the_Prevention_of_Vice_(Gaza_Strip)" title="Committee for the Propagation of Virtue and the Prevention of Vice (Gaza Strip)">Committee for the Propagation of Virtue and the Prevention of Vice (Gaza Strip)</a> in parts of Palestine, and the <a href="/wiki/Basij" title="Basij">Basiji</a> Force in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-rferl_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rferl-95">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hisbah" title="Hisbah">Hisbah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl"><span style="font-size:large;">حسبة</span></span>&#8206; <i>ḥisb(ah)</i>, or hisba) is a historic Islamic doctrine which means "accountability".<sup id="cite_ref-sz_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sz-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Hisbah doctrine holds that it is a religious obligation of every Muslim that he or she report to the ruler (Sultan, government authorities) any wrong behavior of a neighbor or relative that violates sharia or insults Islam. The doctrine states that it is the divinely sanctioned duty of the ruler to intervene when such charges are made, and coercively "command right and forbid wrong" in order to keep everything in order according to sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-lv_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lv-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Jama%27a_al-Islamiyya" title="Al-Jama&#39;a al-Islamiyya">Al-Jama'a al-Islamiyya</a> (considered a terrorist organization) suggest that enforcement of sharia under the Hisbah doctrine is the sacred duty of all Muslims, not just rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-lv_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lv-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The doctrine of Hisbah in Islam may allow a Muslim to accuse another Muslim, ex-Muslim or non-Muslim for beliefs or behavior that harms Islamic society. This principle has been used in countries such as Egypt, Pakistan and others to bring blasphemy charges against <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam" title="Apostasy in Islam">apostates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, sharia was enforced on the Muslim scholar Nasr Abu Zayd, through the doctrine of Hisbah for <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam" title="Apostasy in Islam">apostasy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in Nigeria, after twelve northern Muslim-majority states such as <a href="/wiki/Kano_State" title="Kano State">Kano</a> adopted a sharia-based penal code between 1999 and 2000, hisbah became the allowed method of sharia enforcement where Muslim citizens could police compliance of moral order based on sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In Aceh province of Indonesia, Islamic vigilante activists have invoked Hisbah doctrine to enforce sharia on fellow Muslims as well as demanding that non-Muslims respect sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Hisbah has been used in many Muslim-majority countries to enforce sharia restrictions on <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a> and criticism of Islam over internet and <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_court_proceedings">Legal and court proceedings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legal and court proceedings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Courts"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG/220px-Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG/330px-Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG/440px-Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malacca_State_Syariah_Court.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shariah Court in <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Sharia judicial proceedings have significant differences from other legal traditions, including those in both <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a>. Sharia courts traditionally do not rely on lawyers; <a href="/wiki/Plaintiffs" class="mw-redirect" title="Plaintiffs">plaintiffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Defendants" class="mw-redirect" title="Defendants">defendants</a> represent themselves. Trials are conducted solely by the judge, and there is no jury system. There is no pre-trial <a href="/wiki/Discovery_(law)" title="Discovery (law)">discovery</a> process, and no <a href="/wiki/Cross-examination" title="Cross-examination">cross-examination</a> of witnesses. Unlike common law, judges' verdicts do not set binding <a href="/wiki/Precedents" class="mw-redirect" title="Precedents">precedents</a><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> under the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Stare_decisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Stare decisis">stare decisis</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and unlike civil law, sharia is left to the interpretation in each case and has no formally <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codified</a> universal statutes.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_evidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Rules of evidence">rules of evidence</a> in sharia courts also maintain a distinctive custom of prioritizing oral testimony.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Witnesses, in a sharia court system, must be faithful, that is Muslim.<sup id="cite_ref-av1_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-av1-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Male Muslim witnesses are deemed more reliable than female Muslim witnesses, and non-Muslim witnesses considered unreliable and receive no priority in a sharia court.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In civil cases in some countries, a Muslim woman witness is considered half the worth and reliability than a Muslim man witness.<sup id="cite_ref-unicef2011_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicef2011-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mfdl1_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mfdl1-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In criminal cases, women witnesses are unacceptable in stricter, traditional interpretations of sharia, such as those found in <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> madhhab.<sup id="cite_ref-av1_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-av1-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_cases">Criminal cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Criminal cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A confession, an oath, or the oral testimony of Muslim witnesses are the main evidence admissible, in sharia courts, for hudud crimes, that is the religious crimes of adultery, fornication, rape, accusing someone of illicit sex but failing to prove it, <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam" title="Apostasy in Islam">apostasy</a>, drinking intoxicants and theft.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto2008663_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto2008663-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto200831_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto200831-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Testimony must be from at least two free Muslim male witnesses, or one Muslim male and two Muslim females, who are not related parties and who are of sound mind and reliable character. Testimony to establish the crime of adultery, fornication or rape must be from four Muslim male witnesses, with some <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqhs</a> allowing substitution of up to three male with six female witnesses; however, at least one must be a Muslim male.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Forensic_identification" title="Forensic identification">Forensic evidence</a> (<i>i.e.</i>, fingerprints, ballistics, blood samples, DNA etc.) and other <a href="/wiki/Circumstantial_evidence" title="Circumstantial evidence">circumstantial evidence</a> is likewise rejected in <a href="/wiki/Hudud" title="Hudud">hudud</a> cases in favor of eyewitnesses, a practice which can cause severe difficulties for women plaintiffs in rape cases.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslim jurists have debated whether and when coerced confession and coerced witnesses are acceptable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the Ottoman Criminal Code, the executive officials were allowed to use torture only if the accused had a bad reputation and there were already indications of his guilt, such as when stolen goods were found in his house, if he was accused of grievous bodily harm by the victim or if a criminal during investigation mentioned him as an accomplice.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Press_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Press-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Confessions obtained under torture could not be used as a ground for awarding punishment unless they were corroborated by circumstantial evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Press_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Press-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_cases">Civil cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Civil cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D2%3Averse%3D282">2:282</a> recommends written financial contracts with reliable witnesses, although there is dispute about equality of female testimony.<sup id="cite_ref-mfdl1_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mfdl1-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Marriage is solemnized as a written financial contract, in the presence of two Muslim male witnesses, and it includes a brideprice (<a href="/wiki/Mahr" title="Mahr">Mahr</a>) payable from a Muslim man to a Muslim woman. The brideprice is considered by a sharia court as a form of debt. Written contracts are paramount in sharia courts in the matters of dispute that are debt-related, which includes marriage contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Written contracts in debt-related cases, when notarized by a judge, is deemed more reliable.<sup id="cite_ref-remes1_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-remes1-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In commercial and civil contracts, such as those relating to exchange of merchandise, agreement to supply or purchase goods or property, and others, oral contracts and the testimony of Muslim witnesses triumph over written contracts. Sharia system has held that written commercial contracts may be forged.<sup id="cite_ref-remes1_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-remes1-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timku1_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timku1-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Timur_Kuran" title="Timur Kuran">Timur Kuran</a> states that the treatment of written evidence in religious courts in Islamic regions created an incentive for opaque transactions, and the avoidance of written contracts in economic relations. This led to a continuation of a "largely oral contracting culture" in Muslim-majority nations and communities.<sup id="cite_ref-timku1_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timku1-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In lieu of written evidence, oaths are accorded much greater weight; rather than being used simply to guarantee the truth of ensuing testimony, they are themselves used as evidence. Plaintiffs lacking other evidence to support their claims may demand that defendants take an oath swearing their innocence, refusal thereof can result in a verdict for the plaintiff.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Taking an oath for Muslims can be a grave act; one study of courts in Morocco found that lying litigants would often "maintain their testimony 'right up to the moment of oath-taking and then to stop, refuse the oath, and surrender the case."<sup id="cite_ref-frank_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frank-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Accordingly, defendants are not routinely required to swear before testifying, which would risk casually profaning the Quran should the defendant commit perjury;<sup id="cite_ref-frank_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frank-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> instead oaths are a solemn procedure performed as a final part of the evidence process.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diya">Diya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Diya">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diya_(Islam)" title="Diya (Islam)">Diya (Islam)</a></div>
<p>In classical jurisprudence monetary compensation for bodily harm (<i><a href="/wiki/Diya_(Islam)" title="Diya (Islam)">diya</a></i> or blood money) is assessed differently for different classes of victims. For example, for Muslim women the amount was half that assessed for a Muslim man.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <i>Diya</i> for the death of a free Muslim man is twice as high as for Jewish and Christian victims according to the Maliki and Hanbali madhhabs and three times as high according to Shafi'i rules.<sup id="cite_ref-ame1_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ame1-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Several legals schools assessed <i>diya</i> for <a href="/wiki/Magians" class="mw-redirect" title="Magians">Magians</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Majus" title="Majus">majus</a></i>) at one-fifteenth the value of a free Muslim male.<sup id="cite_ref-ame1_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ame1-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern countries which incorporate classical <i>diya</i> rules into their legal system treat them in different ways. The Pakistan Penal Code modernized the Hanafi doctrine by eliminating distinctions between Muslims and non-Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In Iran, <i>diya</i> for non-Muslim victims professing one of the faiths protected under the constitution (Jews, Christians, and Zoroastrians) was made equal to <i>diya</i> for Muslims in 2004,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> though according to a 2006 US State Department report, the penal code still discriminates against other religious minorities and women.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> According to Human Rights Watch and the US State Department, in Saudi Arabia Jewish or Christian male plaintiffs are entitled to half the amount a Muslim male would receive, while for all other non-Muslim males the proportion is one-sixteenth.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_and_opposition">Support and opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Support and opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support">Support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2013 survey based on interviews of 38,000 Muslims, randomly selected from urban and rural parts in 39 countries using area probability designs, by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Forum" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Forum">Pew Forum on Religion and Public Life</a> found that a majority—in some cases "overwhelming" majority—of Muslims in a number of countries support making sharia the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_land" title="Law of the land">law of the land</a>, including Afghanistan (99%), Iraq (91%), Niger (86%), Malaysia (86%), Pakistan (84%), Morocco (83%), Bangladesh (82%), Egypt (74%), Indonesia (72%), Jordan (71%), Uganda (66%), Ethiopia (65%), Mali (63%), Ghana (58%), and Tunisia (56%).<sup id="cite_ref-pewssp15_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewssp15-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In Muslim regions of Southern-Eastern Europe and Central Asia, the support is less than 50%: Russia (42%), Kyrgyzstan (35%), Tajikistan (27%), Kosovo (20%), Albania (12%), Turkey (12%), Kazakhstan (10%), Azerbaijan (8%). Regarding specific averages, in South Asia, Sharia had 84% favorability rating among the respondents; in Southeast Asia 77%; in the Middle-East/North Africa 74%; in Sub-Saharan Africa 64%; in Southern-Eastern Europe 18%; and in Central Asia 12%.<sup id="cite_ref-pewssp15_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewssp15-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, while most of those who support implementation of sharia favor using it in family and property disputes, fewer supported application of severe punishments such as whippings and cutting off hands, and interpretations of some aspects differed widely.<sup id="cite_ref-pewssp15_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewssp15-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> According to the Pew poll, among Muslims who support making sharia the law of the land, most do not believe that it should be applied to non-Muslims.
In the Muslim-majority countries surveyed this proportion varied between 74% (of 74% in Egypt) and 19% (of 10% in Kazakhstan), as percentage of those who favored making sharia the law of the land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStence201348_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStence201348-142">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Polls demonstrate that for Egyptians, the 'Shariah' is associated with notions of political, social and gender justice.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Williams" title="Rowan Williams">Rowan Williams</a>, the Archbishop of Canterbury, has suggested that Islamic and <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Jewish</a> courts should be integrated into the British legal system alongside ecclesiastical courts to handle marriage and divorce, subject to agreement of all parties and strict requirements for protection of equal rights for women.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-144">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> His reference to the sharia sparked a controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-feldman_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feldman-144">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Phillips,_Baron_Phillips_of_Worth_Matravers" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicholas Phillips, Baron Phillips of Worth Matravers">Nicholas Phillips</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chief_Justice_of_England_and_Wales" title="Lord Chief Justice of England and Wales">Lord Chief Justice of England and Wales</a>, stated that there was "no reason why sharia principles [...] should not be the basis for mediation or other forms of alternative dispute resolution."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 YouGov poll in the United Kingdom found 40% of Muslim students interviewed supported the introduction of sharia into British law for Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Broyde" title="Michael Broyde">Michael Broyde</a>, professor of law at <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a> specializing in <a href="/wiki/Alternative_dispute_resolution" title="Alternative dispute resolution">alternative dispute resolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish law">Jewish law</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> has argued that sharia courts can be integrated into the American religious arbitration system, provided that they adopt appropriate institutional requirements as American rabbinical courts have done.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremism">Extremism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Extremism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_terrorism" title="Islamic terrorism">Islamic terrorism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg/220px-A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg/330px-A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg/440px-A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_public_demonstration_calling_for_Sharia_Islamic_Law_in_Maldives_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A public demonstration calling for Sharia law in <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, September 2014</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Fundamentalists</a>, wishing to return to basic Islamic religious values and law, have in some instances imposed harsh sharia punishments for crimes, curtailed civil rights and violated human rights. Extremists have used the Quran and their own particular version of sharia to justify acts of war and terror against Muslim as well as non-Muslim individuals and governments, using alternate, conflicting interpretations of sharia and their notions of jihad.<sup id="cite_ref-aenexeter_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aenexeter-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale19914_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale19914-150">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale1991100_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale1991100-151">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sharia basis of arguments advocating terrorism is controversial. According to <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a>, "[a]t no time did the classical jurists offer any approval or legitimacy to what we nowadays call terrorism"<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and the terrorist practice of suicide bombing "has no justification in terms of Islamic theology, law or tradition".<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> In the modern era the notion of jihad has lost its jurisprudential relevance and instead gave rise to an ideological and political discourse.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>  For al-Qaeda ideologues, in jihad all means are legitimate, including targeting Muslim non-combatants and the mass killing of non-Muslim civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-aenexeter_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aenexeter-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> According to these interpretations, Islam does not discriminate between military and civilian targets, but rather between Muslims and nonbelievers, whose blood can be legitimately spilled.<sup id="cite_ref-aenexeter_149-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aenexeter-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars of Islam, such as  <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_al-Qaradawi" title="Yusuf al-Qaradawi">Yusuf al-Qaradawi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulaiman_Al-Alwan" title="Sulaiman Al-Alwan">Sulaiman Al-Alwan</a>, have supported suicide attacks against Israeli civilians, arguing that they are army reservists and hence should be considered as soldiers, while <a href="/wiki/Hamid_bin_Abdallah_al-Ali" title="Hamid bin Abdallah al-Ali">Hamid bin Abdallah al-Ali</a> declared that suicide attacks in Chechnya were justified as a "sacrifice".<sup id="cite_ref-aenexeter_149-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aenexeter-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Many prominent Islamic scholars, including al-Qaradawi himself, have issued condemnations of terrorism in general terms.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Abdul-Aziz_ibn_Abdullah_Al_ash-Sheikh" title="Abdul-Aziz ibn Abdullah Al ash-Sheikh">Abdul-Aziz ibn Abdullah Al ash-Sheikh</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Mufti_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Grand Mufti of Saudi Arabia">Grand Mufti of Saudi Arabia</a> has stated that "terrorizing innocent people [...] constitute[s] a form of injustice that cannot be tolerated by Islam", while <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Sayyid_Tantawy" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad Sayyid Tantawy">Muhammad Sayyid Tantawy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Imam_of_al-Azhar" title="Grand Imam of al-Azhar">Grand Imam of al-Azhar</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Grand_Mufti" title="Grand Mufti">Grand Mufti</a> of Egypt has stated that "attacking innocent people is not courageous; it is stupid and will be punished on the Day of Judgment".<sup id="cite_ref-aenexeter_149-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aenexeter-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ban_on_sharia_law" title="Ban on sharia law">Ban on sharia law</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>, sharia has been called a source of "hysteria",<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> "more controversial than ever", the one aspect of Islam that inspires "particular dread".<sup id="cite_ref-kadri-267-8_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kadri-267-8-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> On the Internet, "dozens of self-styled counter-jihadis" emerged to campaign against sharia law, describing it in strict interpretations resembling those of Salafi Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-kadri-267-8_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kadri-267-8-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Also, fear of sharia law and of "the ideology of extremism" among Muslims reportedly spread to mainstream conservative <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-TPM-9-23-2010_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPM-9-23-2010-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a> won ovations calling for a federal ban on sharia law.<sup id="cite_ref-TPM-9-23-2010_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPM-9-23-2010-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
The issue of "liberty versus Sharia" was called a "momentous civilisational debate" by right-wing pundit <a href="/wiki/Diana_West" title="Diana West">Diana West</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
In 2008 in Britain, the future Prime Minister (<a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>) declared his opposition to "any expansion of Sharia law in the UK."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-26-3-2008_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-26-3-2008-162">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In Germany, in 2014, the Interior Minister (<a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Maizi%C3%A8re" title="Thomas de Maizière">Thomas de Maizière</a>) told a newspaper (<i><a href="/wiki/Bild" title="Bild">Bild</a></i>), "Sharia law is not tolerated on German soil."<sup id="cite_ref-dw_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dw-163">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some countries and jurisdictions have explicit bans on sharia law. In Canada, for example, sharia law has been explicitly banned in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> by a 2005 unanimous vote of the National Assembly,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> while the province of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> allows family law disputes to be arbitrated only under Ontario law.<sup id="cite_ref-CHOKSI-14-3-2012_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHOKSI-14-3-2012-165">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In the U.S., opponents of Sharia have sought to ban it from being considered in courts, where it has been routinely used alongside traditional Jewish and Catholic laws to decide legal, business, and family disputes subject to contracts drafted with reference to such laws, as long as they do not violate secular law or the U.S. constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> After failing to gather support for a federal law making observing Sharia a felony punishable by up to 20 years in prison, anti-Sharia activists have focused on state legislatures.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> By 2014, bills aimed against use of Sharia have been introduced in 34 states and passed in 11.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> These bills have generally referred to banning foreign or religious law in order to thwart legal challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Jan Michiel Otto, Professor of Law and Governance in Developing Countries at <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a>, "[a]nthropological research shows that people in local communities often do not distinguish clearly whether and to what extent their norms and practices are based on local tradition, tribal custom, or religion. Those who adhere to a confrontational view of sharia tend to ascribe many undesirable practices to sharia and religion overlooking custom and culture, even if high-ranking religious authorities have stated the opposite."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto200830_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto200830-166">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg/220px-Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg/330px-Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg/440px-Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2499" data-file-height="2140" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ground_Zero_Mosque_Protesters_11.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A protester opposing the <a href="/wiki/Park51" title="Park51">Park51</a> project, carries an anti-sharia sign.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compatibility_with_democracy">Compatibility with democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Compatibility with democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics" title="Islamic ethics">Islamic ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam and democracy">Islam and democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">Shura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">Ijma</a></div>
<p>Ali Khan states that "constitutional orders founded on the principles of sharia are fully compatible with democracy, provided that religious minorities are protected and the incumbent Islamic leadership remains committed to the right to recall".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars say sharia is not compatible with democracy, particularly where the country's constitution demands separation of religion and the democratic state.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Courts in non-Muslim-majority nations have generally ruled against the implementation of sharia, both in jurisprudence and within a community context, based on sharia's religious background. In Muslim-majority nations, sharia has wide support with some exceptions.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> For example, in 1998 the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_Turkey" title="Constitutional Court of Turkey">Constitutional Court of Turkey</a> banned and dissolved Turkey's <a href="/wiki/Welfare_Party" title="Welfare Party">Refah Party</a> on the grounds that "Democracy is the antithesis of Sharia", the latter of which Refah sought to introduce.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On appeal by Refah the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> determined that "sharia is incompatible with the fundamental principles of democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Refah's sharia-based notion of a "plurality of legal systems, grounded on religion" was ruled to contravene the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms</a>. It was determined that it would "do away with the State's role as the guarantor of individual rights and freedoms" and "infringe the principle of non-discrimination between individuals as regards their enjoyment of public freedoms, which is one of the fundamental principles of democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several major, predominantly Muslim countries have criticized the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (UDHR) for its perceived failure to take into account the cultural and religious context of non-<a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> countries. Iran declared in the UN assembly that UDHR was "a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> understanding of the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> tradition", which could not be implemented by Muslims without trespassing the Islamic law.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Islamic scholars and Islamist political parties consider 'universal human rights' arguments as imposition of a non-Muslim culture on Muslim people, a disrespect of customary cultural practices and of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-as1999_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-as1999-179">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>, a group representing all Muslim-majority nations, met in Cairo to respond to the UDHR, then adopted the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Declaration_on_Human_Rights_in_Islam" title="Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam">Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ann_Elizabeth_Mayer" title="Ann Elizabeth Mayer">Ann Elizabeth Mayer</a> points to notable absences from the Cairo Declaration: provisions for democratic principles, protection for religious freedom, freedom of association and freedom of the press, as well as equality in rights and equal protection under the law. Article 24 of the Cairo declaration states that "all the rights and freedoms stipulated in this Declaration are subject to the Islamic <i>shari'a</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-mayer_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayer-183">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Free_Inquiry" title="Free Inquiry">Free Inquiry</a></i> summarized the criticism of the Cairo Declaration in an editorial: "We are deeply concerned with the changes to the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> by a coalition of Islamic states within the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> that wishes to prohibit any criticism of religion and would thus protect Islam's limited view of human rights. In view of the conditions inside the Islamic Republic of Iran, Egypt, Pakistan, Saudi Arabia, the Sudan, Syria, Bangladesh, Iraq, and Afghanistan, we should expect that at the top of their human rights agenda would be to rectify the legal inequality of women, the suppression of political dissent, the curtailment of free expression, the persecution of ethnic minorities and religious dissenters&#160;– in short, protecting their citizens from egregious human rights violations. Instead, they are worrying about protecting Islam."<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/H._Patrick_Glenn" title="H. Patrick Glenn">H. Patrick Glenn</a> states that sharia is structured around the concept of mutual obligations of a collective, and it considers individual human rights as potentially disruptive and unnecessary to its revealed code of mutual obligations. In giving priority to this religious collective rather than individual liberty, the Islamic law justifies the formal inequality of individuals (women, non-Islamic people).<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Bassam Tibi states that sharia framework and human rights are incompatible.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Abdel al-Hakeem Carney, in contrast, states that sharia is misunderstood from a failure to distinguish <i>sharia</i> from <i>siyasah</i> (politics).<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_speech">Freedom of speech</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Freedom of speech">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_blasphemy" title="Islam and blasphemy">Islam and blasphemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jyllands-Posten_Muhammad_cartoons_controversy" title="Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy">Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy_laws_of_the_Islamic_Republic_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Blasphemy laws of the Islamic Republic of Iran">Blasphemy laws of the Islamic Republic of Iran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy_law_in_Pakistan" title="Blasphemy law in Pakistan">Blasphemy law in Pakistan</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Declaration_on_Human_Rights_in_Islam" title="Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam">Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam</a> conditions free speech with sharia law: Article 22(a) of the Declaration states that "Everyone shall have the right to express his opinion freely in such manner as would not be contrary to the principles of the Shariah."<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Islam_and_blasphemy" title="Islam and blasphemy">Blasphemy in Islam</a> is any form of cursing, questioning or annoying God, Muhammad or anything considered sacred in Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-khan_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khan-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The sharia of various Islamic schools of jurisprudence specify different punishment for blasphemy against Islam, by Muslims and non-Muslims, ranging from imprisonment, fines, flogging, amputation, hanging, or beheading.<sup id="cite_ref-khan_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-khan-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, sharia allows non-Muslims to escape death by converting and becoming a devout follower of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Blasphemy, as interpreted under sharia, is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Muslim-majority nations have petitioned the United Nations to limit "freedom of speech" because "unrestricted and disrespectful opinion against Islam creates hatred".<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Other nations, in contrast, consider blasphemy laws as violation of "freedom of speech",<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> stating that freedom of expression is essential to empowering both Muslims and non-Muslims, and point to the abuse of blasphemy laws, where hundreds, often members of religious minorities, are being lynched, killed and incarcerated in Muslim-majority nations, on flimsy accusations of insulting Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Freedom_of_thought.2C_conscience_and_religion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_thought,_conscience_and_religion">Freedom of thought, conscience and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Freedom of thought, conscience and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG/500px-Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG.png" width="500" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG/750px-Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG/1000px-Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apostasy_laws_in_2013.SVG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries that criminalize <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_from_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostasy from Islam">apostasy from Islam</a> as of 2013. Some Muslim-majority countries impose the death penalty or a prison sentence for apostasy from Islam, or ban non-Muslims from proselytizing .<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to the United Nations' <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, every human has the right to freedom of thought, conscience and religion; this right includes freedom to change their religion or belief. Sharia has been criticized for not recognizing this human right. According to scholars<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> of Islamic law, the applicable rules for religious conversion under sharia are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>If a person converts to Islam, or is born and raised as a Muslim, then he or she will have full rights of citizenship in an Islamic state.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Leaving Islam is a sin and a religious crime. Once any man or woman is officially classified as Muslim, because of birth or religious conversion, he or she will be subject to the death penalty if he or she becomes an <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam" title="Apostasy in Islam">apostate</a>, that is, abandons his or her faith in Islam in order to become an atheist, agnostic or to convert to another religion. Before executing the death penalty, sharia demands that the individual be offered one chance to return to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>If a person has never been a Muslim, and is not a <a href="/wiki/Kafir" title="Kafir">kafir</a> (infidel, unbeliever), he or she can live in an Islamic state by accepting to be a <a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">dhimmi</a>, or under a special permission called <i>aman</i>. As a dhimmi or under aman, he or she will suffer certain limitations of rights as a subject of an Islamic state, and will not enjoy complete legal equality with Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>If a person has never been a Muslim, and is a kafir (infidel, unbeliever), sharia demands that he or she should be offered the choice to convert to Islam and become a Muslim; if he or she rejects the offer, he or she may become a dhimmi. Failure to pay the tax may lead the non-Muslim to either be enslaved, killed or ransomed if captured.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>According to sharia theory, conversion of disbelievers and non-Muslims to Islam is encouraged as a religious duty for all Muslims, and leaving Islam (apostasy), expressing contempt for Islam (blasphemy), and religious conversion of Muslims is prohibited.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Not all Islamic scholars agree with this interpretation of sharia theory. In practice, as of 2011, 20 Muslim-majority nations had laws declaring apostasy from Islam as illegal and a criminal offense. Such laws are incompatible with the UDHR's requirement of freedom of thought, conscience and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> In another 2013 report based on international survey of religious attitudes, more than 50% of Muslim population in 6 out of 49 Muslim-majority countries supported death penalty for any Muslim who leaves Islam (apostasy).<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> However it is also shown that the majority of Muslims in the 43 nations surveyed did not agree with this interpretation of sharia.
</p><p>Some scholars claim sharia allows religious freedom because a Qur'anic verse teaches, "there is no compulsion in religion."<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars claim sharia recognizes only one proper religion, considers apostasy as sin punishable with death, and members of other religions as kafir (<a href="/wiki/Infidel" title="Infidel">infidel</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> or hold that sharia demands that all apostates and kafir must be put to death, enslaved or be ransomed.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (May 2016)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhadduri1955&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2016&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhadduri1955[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2016]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2016)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-217">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Yet other scholars suggest that sharia has become a product of human interpretation and inevitably leads to disagreements about the “precise contents of the Shari'a." In the end, then, what is being applied is not sharia, but what a particular group of clerics and government decide is sharia. It is these differing interpretations of sharia that explain why many Muslim-majority countries have laws that restrict and criminalize apostasy, proselytism and their citizens' freedom of conscience and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBT_rights">LGBT rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: LGBT rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_in_Islam" title="LGBT in Islam">LGBT in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg/220px-World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg.png" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg/330px-World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg/440px-World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1035" data-file-height="531" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_laws_pertaining_to_homosexual_relationships_and_expression.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Same-sex intercourse illegal: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #800000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">Death penalty</a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e73e21; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Up to <a href="/wiki/Life_in_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Life in prison">life in prison</a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ec8028; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Imprisonment</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f9dc36; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Unenforced penalty</div></div></div></div>
<p>Homosexual intercourse is illegal in sharia law, though the prescribed penalties differ from one school of jurisprudence to another. For example, some Muslim-majority countries impose the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">death penalty</a> for acts perceived as <a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">sodomy</a> and homosexual activities: <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> and in other Muslim-majority countries such as <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> province of <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> same-sex sexual acts are illegal,<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> and LGBT people regularly face violence and discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Islam" title="Women in Islam">Women in Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_domestic_violence" title="Islam and domestic violence">Islam and domestic violence</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_violence">Domestic violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Domestic violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many claim sharia law encourages domestic violence against women, when a husband suspects <i><a href="/wiki/Nushuz" class="mw-redirect" title="Nushuz">nushuz</a></i> (disobedience, disloyalty, rebellion, ill conduct) in his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars claim wife beating, for <i>nashizah</i>, is not consistent with modern perspectives of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the verses of the Quran relating to permissibility of domestic violence is <a href="/wiki/An-Nisa,_34" title="An-Nisa, 34">Surah 4:34</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Sharia has been criticized for ignoring women's rights in domestic abuse cases.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Musawah" title="Musawah">Musawah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_Against_Women" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women">CEDAW</a>, KAFA and other organizations have proposed ways to modify sharia-inspired laws to improve women's rights in Muslim-majority nations, including women's rights in domestic abuse cases.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_status_laws_and_child_marriage">Personal status laws and child marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Personal status laws and child marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Shari'a is the basis for personal status laws in most Islamic-majority nations. These personal status laws determine <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">rights of women</a> in matters of marriage, divorce and child custody. A 2011 <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> report concludes that sharia law provisions are discriminatory against women from a human rights perspective. In legal proceedings under sharia law, a woman’s testimony is worth half of a man’s before a court.<sup id="cite_ref-unicef2011_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicef2011-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Except for Iran<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, Lebanon<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and Bahrain<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which allow child marriages<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, the civil codes in Islamic majority countries do not allow <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a> of girls. However, with sharia personal status laws, sharia courts in all these nations have the power to override the civil code. The religious courts permit girls less than 18 years old to marry. As of 2011, child marriages are common in a few Middle Eastern countries, accounting for 1 in 6 of all marriages in Egypt and 1 in 3 marriages in Yemen. <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> and other studies state that the top five nations in the world with highest observed child marriage rates – Niger (75%), Chad (72%), Mali (71%), Bangladesh (64%), Guinea (63%) – are Islamic-majority countries where the personal laws for Muslims are sharia-based.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Cairo_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Cairo speech">Cairo speech</a>, President Obama spoke out against child marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rape is considered a crime in all countries, but sharia courts in Bahrain, Iraq, Jordan, Libya, Morocco, Syria and Tunisia in some cases allow a rapist to escape punishment by marrying his victim, while in other cases the victim who complains is often prosecuted with the crime of <i><a href="/wiki/Zina" title="Zina">Zina</a></i> (adultery).<sup id="cite_ref-unicef2011_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicef2011-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Women.27s_right_to_property_and_consent"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_right_to_property_and_consent">Women's right to property and consent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Women&#039;s right to property and consent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sharia grants women the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">right to inherit property</a> from other family members, and these rights are detailed in the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale199149_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrieChippindale199149-244">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> A woman's inheritance is unequal and less than a man's, and dependent on many factors.<sup>[<a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D4%3Averse%3D12">4:12</a>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-davidpowers_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidpowers-245">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> For instance, a daughter's inheritance is usually half that of her brother's.<sup>[<a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D4%3Averse%3D11">4:11</a>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-davidpowers_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davidpowers-245">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the 20th century, Islamic law granted Muslim women certain legal rights, such as the right to own property received as <a href="/wiki/Mahr" title="Mahr">Mahr</a> (brideprice) at her marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-247">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> However, Islamic law does not grant non-Muslim women the same legal rights as the few it did grant Muslim women. Sharia recognizes the basic inequality between master and women slave, between free women and slave women, between Believers and non-Believers, as well as their unequal rights.<sup id="cite_ref-blbr_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blbr-248">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Sharia authorized the institution of slavery, using the words <i>abd</i> (slave) and the phrase <i>ma malakat aymanukum</i> ("that which your right hand owns") to refer to women slaves, seized as captives of war.<sup id="cite_ref-blbr_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blbr-248">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Under Islamic law, Muslim men could have sexual relations with female captives and slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-alik_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alik-252">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Slave women under sharia did not have a right to own property or to move freely.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Sharia, in Islam's history, provided a religious foundation for enslaving non-Muslim women (and men), but allowed for the <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumission</a> of slaves. However, manumission required that the non-Muslim slave first convert to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-pl1_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pl1-255">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> A non-Muslim slave woman who bore children to her Muslim master became legally free upon her master's death, and her children were presumed to be Muslims like their father, in Africa<sup id="cite_ref-pl1_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pl1-255">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting with the 20th century, Western legal systems evolved to expand women's rights, but women's rights under Islamic law have remained tied to the Quran, hadiths and their fundamentalist interpretation as sharia by Islamic jurists.<sup id="cite_ref-alik_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alik-252">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parallels_with_Western_legal_systems">Parallels with Western legal systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Parallels with Western legal systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Early Islamic law developed a number of legal concepts that anticipated similar such concepts that later appeared in English <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makdisi_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makdisi-259">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Devichand_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devichand-260">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Similarities exist between the royal English <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contract</a> protected by the action of <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a> and the Islamic <i>Aqd</i>, between the English <a href="/wiki/Assize_of_novel_disseisin" title="Assize of novel disseisin">assize of novel disseisin</a> and the Islamic <i>Istihqaq</i>, and between the English <a href="/wiki/Jury" title="Jury">jury</a> and the Islamic <i>Lafif</i> in classical Maliki jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-Makdisi_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makdisi-259">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Law_school"
