title="Law school">law schools</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Inns_of_Court" title="Inns of Court">Inns of Court</a> also parallel <a href="/wiki/Madrasah" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrasah">Madrasahs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makdisi_259-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makdisi-259">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The methodology of legal <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a> and reasoning by <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogy</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">Qiyas</a></i>) are also similar in both the Islamic and common law systems,<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> as are the English <a href="/wiki/Trust_law" title="Trust law">trust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agency_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Agency (law)">agency</a> institutions to the Islamic <i><a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">Waqf</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hawala" title="Hawala">Hawala</a></i> institutions, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Devichand_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devichand-260">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Elements of Islamic law also have other parallels in Western legal systems. For example, the influence of Islam on the development of an international law of the sea can be discerned alongside that of the Roman influence.<sup id="cite_ref-Tai_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tai-265">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Makdisi states Islamic law also parallels the legal scholastic system in the West, which gave rise to the modern university system.<sup id="cite_ref-G-Makdisi_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-Makdisi-266">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> He writes that the triple status of <i><a href="/wiki/Faqih" class="mw-redirect" title="Faqih">faqih</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Master_of_Laws" title="Master of Laws">master of law</a>"), <i><a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">mufti</a></i> ("professor of <a href="/wiki/Fatw%C4%81" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatwā">legal opinions</a>") and <i>mudarris</i> ("teacher"), conferred by the classical Islamic legal degree, had its equivalents in the medieval Latin terms <i><a href="/wiki/Magister_(degree)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magister (degree)">magister</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_(title)" title="Doctor (title)">doctor</a></i>, respectively, although they all came to be used synonymously in both East and West.<sup id="cite_ref-G-Makdisi_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-Makdisi-266">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Makdisi suggests that the medieval European doctorate, <i>licentia docendi</i> was modeled on the Islamic degree <i>ijazat al-tadris wa-l-ifta’</i>, of which it is a word-for-word translation, with the term <i>ifta’</i> (issuing of fatwas) omitted.<sup id="cite_ref-G-Makdisi_266-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-Makdisi-266">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> He also argues that these systems shared fundamental freedoms: the freedom of a professor to profess his personal opinion and the freedom of a student to pass judgement on what he is learning.<sup id="cite_ref-G-Makdisi_266-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G-Makdisi-266">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are differences between Islamic and Western legal systems. For example, sharia classically recognizes only <a href="/wiki/Natural_persons" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural persons">natural persons</a>, and never developed the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Legal_person" title="Legal person">legal person</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporation</a>, i.e., a legal entity that <a href="/wiki/Limited_liability" title="Limited liability">limits the liabilities</a> of its managers, shareholders, and employees; exists beyond the lifetimes of its founders; and that can own assets, sign contracts, and appear in court through representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Interest prohibitions imposed secondary costs by discouraging record keeping and delaying the introduction of modern accounting.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Such factors, according to Timur Kuran, have played a significant role in retarding economic development in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sharia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"What theology is for the Christian, law is for the Muslim."<sup id="cite_ref-Smith-1957-57_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-1957-57-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> referenced in <sup id="cite_ref-Forte-1978-2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forte-1978-2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While the advocacy of <i>hudud</i> punishments has gained symbolic importance, and in theory often involved rejection of the stringent traditional restrictions on their application, in practice, in those few countries where they have been reintroduced, they have often been used sparingly or not at all. Their application has varied depending on local political climate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVikør2014_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVikør2014-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtto200820_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtto200820-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">“... the essential features of old Muhammadan jurisprudence, such as the idea of the `living tradition` of the ancient schools of law; a body of common doctrine expressing the earliest effort to systematize;  legal maxims which often reflect a slightly later stage, and an important nucleus of legal traditions ... it is safe to say that [this] Muhammadan legal science started in the later part of the Umaiyad period, taking the legal practice of the time as its raw material and endorsing, modifying, or rejecting it, ...” ,<sup id="cite_ref-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-190_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-190-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">“In the time of Shafi’i, traditions from the Prophet were already recognized as one of the material bases of Muhammadan law. Their position in the ancient schools of law was, as we have seen, much less certain.”<sup id="cite_ref-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-40_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-40-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">another example is that an early major works of fiqh — <a href="/wiki/Muwatta_Imam_Malik" title="Muwatta Imam Malik">Muwatta Imam Malik</a> (edited by Shaibani) — contains 429 ahadith by Muhammad but  750 by the <a href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">Companions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabi%E2%80%98un" title="Tabi‘un">Successors</a> and others,<sup id="cite_ref-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-22_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-22-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> in contrast to later works by al-Bukhari, Muslim, etc. that contain only ahadith by Muhammad</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">”a great many traditions in the classical and other collections were put into circulation only after Shafi'i's time;
the first considerable body of legal traditions from the Prophet originated towards the middle of the second century, …”<sup id="cite_ref-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-4_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schacht-OoMJ-1959-4-42">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://caselaw.findlaw.com/data2/delawarestatecases/493-2003.pdf"><i>Saudi Arabia Basic Industries Corp. v. Mobil Yanbu Petrochemical Co.</i>, Supreme Court of Delaware, January 14, 2005 p. 52</a>. "The Saudi law system differs in critically important respects from the system of legal thought employed by the common law countries, including the United States. Perhaps most significant is that Islamic law does not embrace the common law system of binding precedent and <i>stare decisis</i>. In Saudi Arabia, judicial decisions are not in themselves a source of law, and with minor exceptions, court decisions in Saudi Arabia are not published or even open to public inspection."</span>

"the literacy rate in the Ottoman Empire in 1900 was between five and ten percent".</span>

Cambridge University Press. pp.&#160;16–17. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0521784306" title="Special:BookSources/978-0521784306">978-0521784306</a>. <q>The religious requirement that new slaves be pagans and need for continued imports to maintain slave population made Africa an important source of slaves for the Islamic world. (...) In Islamic tradition, slavery was perceived as a means of converting non-Muslims. One task of the master was religious instruction and theoretically Muslims could not be enslaved. Conversion (of a non-Muslim to Islam) did not automatically lead to emancipation, but assimilation into Muslim society was deemed a prerequisite for emancipation.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Transformations+in+Slavery%3A+A+History+of+Slavery+in+Africa&amp;rft.pages=16-17&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.isbn=978-0521784306&amp;rft.aulast=Lovejoy&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASharia" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Jean Pierre Angenot;  et al. (2008). <i>Uncovering the History of Africans in Asia</i>. Brill Academic. p.&#160;60. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-9004162914" title="Special:BookSources/978-9004162914">978-9004162914</a>. <q>Islam imposed upon the Muslim master an obligation to convert non-Muslim slaves and become members of the greater Muslim society. Indeed, the daily observation of well defined Islamic religious rituals was the outward manifestation of conversion without which emancipation was impossible.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Uncovering+the+History+of+Africans+in+Asia&amp;rft.pages=60&amp;rft.pub=Brill+Academic&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-9004162914&amp;rft.au=Jean+Pierre+Angenot&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASharia" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span>
